Upon starting, MEDUSA was configured to execute commands and executables listed under \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e/usr/lib/locate/.boot.sh\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e as follows:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e/usr/sbin/libvird\r\n/usr/bin/NetworkManage\r\nchcon -t sshd_tmp_t /var/run/cron.data\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe executables and the command constitute a component of the threat actor's attempt to hijack SSH connections with the objective of acquiring SSH credentials. Analysis of the executables and their attempts is discussed later in this report.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMalware Leveraging Trusted Third Parties as C2 Channel\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe threat actor was observed deploying malware, including MOPSLED and RIFLESPINE, that leverages trusted third parties like GitHub and Google Drive as C2 channels while relying on the rootkits for persistence.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMOPSLED\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMOPSLED is a shellcode-based modular backdoor that has the capability to communicate over HTTP or a custom binary protocol over TCP to its C2 server. The core functionality of MOPSLED involves expanding its capabilities by retrieving plugins from the C2 server. MOPSLED also uses a custom ChaCha20 encryption algorithm to decrypt embedded and external configuration files.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant observed sharing of MOPSLED between other Chinese cyber espionage groups including APT41. Mandiant considered MOPSLED to be an evolution of CROSSWALK, which can act as a network proxy.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant observed UNC3886 deploy the Linux variant, identified as MOPSLED.LINUX, on vCenter servers and a small number of the compromised endpoints where REPTILE already existed. MOPSLED.LINUX appeared to be used only as an initial malware deployed after gaining successful access since the malware does not have rootkit-like capabilities that could evade detection.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMOPSLED.LINUX was developed to communicate with a dead-drop URL to retrieve an actual C2 address. The sample associated with UNC3886 was observed sending HTTP GET requests to \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ehttps://cyberponke.github[.]io/*\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e. The response was decrypted using the ChaCha20 cipher to obtain the actual C2 IP address. Further communications are implemented as a custom binary protocol similar to HTTP/S.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eRIFLESPINE\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eRIFLESPINE is a cross-platform backdoor that leverages Google Drive to transfer files and execute commands. It adopts the CryptoPP library to implement the AES algorithm to encrypt and decrypt the data transmitted between an affected machine and the threat actor.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eTo instruct RIFLESPINE, the threat actor creates an encrypted file on Google Drive with instructions for RIFLESPINE that is then executed by the malware on the target endpoint. The target endpoint's MAC address must appear in the filename when it is created. The file is downloaded, RIFLESPINE downloads and decrypts the file, and executes the instructions. The executions' outputs will be encrypted, stored in a temporary file, and then uploaded to Google Drive once more. The following instructions are available for execution:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eDownload file with \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eget\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e command.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eUpload file with \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eput\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e command.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eSet next call out time in milliseconds with \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003esettime.\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eExecution arbitrary commands with \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e/bin/sh.\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eUNC3886 deployed RIFLESPINE with \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/prasmussen/gdrive\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ean open-source Google Drive CLI client\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e. It is reasonable to assume that the threat actor abandoned the idea of using MOPSLED.LINUX and RIFLESPINE, which do not have rootkit functionality, as backdoors because predictable communications to GitHub and Google Drive services from virtual machine servers, rather than workstations, could raise suspicions.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eSubverting Accesses With Backdoored Applications\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant observed UNC3886 relying heavily on collecting and utilizing valid credentials for lateral movement between guest virtual machines running on the compromised VMware ESXi. The following section describes different techniques used by the threat actor to collect and abuse valid credentials.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eBackdoored SSH Executables\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAfter gaining access to the guest virtual machines, either through the collection of \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003evpxuser\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e credentials or by exploiting CVE-2023-20867 in conjunction with VMware Guest Operations abuse to facilitate malicious file transfer and execution, UNC3886 was observed deploying backdoored SSH clients and daemons. The purpose of these malicious components was the interception and collection of credentials within an XOR-encrypted text file.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAnalysis of the compromised SSH client located at \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e/usr/bin/ssh\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e exposed modifications by the threat actor to the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003euserauth_passwd()\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e function, which governs password-based authentication. These modifications (detailed in Figure 2) introduce instructions designed to harvest SSH credentials from outgoing connections. This marked the point at which a supply chain attack scenario became conceivable.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe existence of NMAP scan logs suggests that there is connectivity from the jump server to the foreign networks, although accessibility requires legitimate credentials. The final clue aligned with this assumption as the ongoing investigation uncovered malicious activities on a TACACS+ server accessible from the jump server.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eTACACS is a network protocol used in computer networking for providing centralized authentication, authorization, and accounting (AAA) service. TACACS+ represents an enhanced and more robust version of the original TACACS protocol. Network appliances employ TACACS+ for security and access control, ensuring that authenticated users are authorized to execute actions that are monitored for auditing purposes.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAn unauthorized access to a system functioning as an authentication server like a TACACS+ server is an absolute security nightmare. The threat actor could access or manipulate user credentials and authorization policies stored within its database. Accountability of TACACS+ would also be affected as the threat actor could tamper with the accounting logs stored on the TACACS+ server, covering their tracks and concealing malicious activities.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe following sections describe actions performed by the threat actor to extend their access to the target network appliances.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eCapturing TACACS+ Credentials with LOOKOVER\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe threat actor's first attempt to extend their access to the network appliances by targeting the TACACS server was the use of LOOKOVER. LOOKOVER is a sniffer written in C that processes TACACS+ authentication packets, performs decryption, and writes its contents to a specified file path. LOOKOVER uses the publicly available libpcap library to sniff TCP packets.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe threat actor deployed LOOKOVER on the TACACS+ server at \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e/usr/sbin/au\u0026lt;unique_keyword\u0026gt;ditd\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e. The process crashed when attempting to encrypt extracted credentials before writing to disks, and this could influence the threat actor to employ another approach to target TACACS+.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eBackdoored TACACS+ Binary\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eOn the same TACACS+ server identified with LOOKOVER, Mandiant observed the threat actor replaced the legitimate \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e/usr/bin/tac_plus\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e, which is the TACACS+ daemon for Linux, with a malicious version containing credential logging functionality.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe malicious version of \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e/usr/bin/tac_plus\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e was modified with a new function responsible for logging TACACS+ credentials to \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e/var/log/tacu\u0026lt;unique_keyword\u0026gt;cs.log\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e. The function was inserted in the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003everify()\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e after the password was validated and in the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003epasswd_file_verify()\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e, which is responsible for confirming a credential after the password is confirmed to be correct. The captured credential record is XOR-ed with \u003c/span\u003e\u003ccode style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e0xEF\u003c/code\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e before appending to the credential log file.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 6: Malicious function within tac_plus for capturing credentials\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig6.max-600x600.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig6.max-600x600.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 7: Backdoored authentication function of tac_plus\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig7.max-700x700.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc3886-cloaked-covert-fig7.max-700x700.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe Family of VMCI Backdoors\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eMandiant discovered a new variant of backdoors leveraging the Virtual Machine Communication Interface (VMCI) as a communication protocol. The VMCI backdoors could facilitate either guest-to-guest or host-to-guest communications to achieve command execution. See \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/vmware-esxi-zero-day-bypass/\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ethe overview of the attacker's use of ESXi Hypervisor VMCI communications\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e for more information.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eVIRTUALSHINE\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e is a simple VMware VMCI sockets-based backdoor that provides access to a bash shell. VIRTUALSHINE connects to a specified target, which streams the bash pty.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eVIRTUALPIE\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e is a backdoor written in Python that spawns a demonized IPv6 listener on a hard-coded TCP port. It supports file transfer, arbitrary command execution, and reverse shell capabilities. It communicates using a custom protocol and the data is encrypted using RC4.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eVIRTUALSPHERE\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e is the controller part of a simple VMCI-based backdoor. The malware transmits the second command-line argument over the VMCI socket to the server running inside the target VM.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eWe plan to release technical details of the VMCI backdoors in a future blog post.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eCampaign 23-022 and Indicators of Compromise\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eSince March 2023, we have tracked UNC3886 activity leveraging zero-day exploits for Fortinet and VMware technologies as part of \u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eCampaign 23-022\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e in \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/security/products/mandiant-threat-intelligence\"\u003eMandiant Advantage\u003c/a\u003e for our customers. The majority of organizations that Mandiant has responded to or identified as targets through our own analysis have been located in the North America, Southeast Asia, or Oceania regions. However, we have also identified evidence of additional victims located in Europe, Africa, and other parts of Asia. Industries that Mandiant has observed being targeted are those typically observed in espionage operations, namely governments, telecommunications, technology, aerospace and defense, and energy and utility sectors. \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eTo assist the wider community in hunting and identifying activity outlined in this
